hidden"><div data-badge-details="right" data-badge-type="donut" data-hide-no-mentions="false" data-condensed="true" class="altmetric-embed"></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">18</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> is recognized as a promising cathode for high energy density sodium-ion batteries due to its high average potential of ∼3.95 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). A high-voltage-resistant electrolyte is of high importance due to the long duration of 4.2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) when improving cyclability. Herein, a targeted electrolyte containing additives with two –C≡N groups like succinonitrile has been designed. In this design, one –C≡N group is accessible to the solvation sheath and enables the other –C≡N in dinitrile being exposed and subsequently squeezed into the electric double layer. Then, the squeezed –C≡N group is prone to a preferential adsorption on the electrode surface prior to the exposed –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> in Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and oxidized to construct a stable and electrically insulating interface enriched CN<sup>–</sup>/NCO<sup>–</sup>/Na<sub>3</sub>N. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>-based sodium-ion batteries within a high-voltage of 2–4.3 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) can accordingly achieve an excellent cycling stability (e.g., 95.07% reversible capacity at 1 C for 1,5-dicyanopentane and 98.4% at 2 C and 93.0% reversible capacity at 5 C for succinonitrile after 1000 cycles). This work proposes a new way to design high-voltage electrolytes for high energy density sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D12519%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMingqin%2BJiang%252C%2BTianyu%2BLi%252C%2BYanling%2BQiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D146%26issueNum%3D18%26contentID%3Djacs.4c00702%26title%3DElectrolyte%2BDesign%2Bwith%2BDual%2B%25E2%2580%2593C%25E2%2589%25A1N%2BGroups%2BContaining%2BAdditives%2Bto%2BEnable%2BHigh-Voltage%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25292F3-Based%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0002-7863%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Djacsat%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D12529%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292197&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Additives"> Additives </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes"> Electrolytes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291306&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Solvation"> Solvation </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292017&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Solvents"> Solvents </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), benefiting from element abundance and low cost, are regarded as promising candidates for medium- and low-speed electric vehicles and large-scale energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the challenges in energy and power density resulting from the large atomic mass and radius necessitate the development of cathode materials with high voltage, high specific capacity, and favorable conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Among various candidates, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF<sub>3</sub>) has been widely investigated owing to the high average potential of ∼3.95 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and theoretical specific capacity (128 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 4 to 7">(4−7)</a> Accordingly, NVPF<sub>3</sub> can deliver a high theoretical energy density of ∼507 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>, approximative to that of commercial LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (530 W h kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Resembling the commonly accepted cognition, NVPF<sub>3</sub> also has a concern in unsatisfied cycle life with rapid capacity fading. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> Several research studies have been focused on clarifying the capacity fading mechanism of polyanionic compounds, especially NVPF<sub>3</sub>. One of the viewpoints is F element dissolution from NVPF<sub>3</sub> inducing capacity degradation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> The exfoliation occurring between the active material and collector caused by HF corrosion is mostly responsible for the capacity decay, where HF is generated through F<sup>–</sup> dissolution from NVPF<sub>3</sub> aroused by trace H<sub>2</sub>O upon cycling. Some strategies, such as restraining the volatilization of HF by optimizing synthesis process, supplying the loss of fluorine by element doping, etc., were proposed to boost the cycling performance by stabilizing fluorine or avoiding fluorine loss. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> Another view is that the migration of V-to-Na (1) site causes destructive structure of polyanionic host upon cycling based on Na<sub>3</sub>VCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVCP). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> In regard to this, the substitution of V<sup>3+</sup> through some inactive elements such as Mn<sup>3+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>3+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, etc. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 10 and 18 to 21">(10,18−21)</a> has been proposed to support and accordingly improve the cycling performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">However, the above interpretation does not work very well to suppress the degradation of NVPF<sub>3</sub> due to the excellent structural stability of polyanion-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 22 to 26">(22−26)</a> and negligible capacity loss aroused shedding of active materials induced by trace H<sub>2</sub>O with the thorough drying of electrodes. So, corresponding studies about the above two views have shown slight improvement of stability by modification for NVPF<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref27" aria-label="reference 10, 13 to 15, 18 to 20 and 27">(10,13−15,18−20,27)</a> Simultaneously, the continuous oxidation of electrolytes exceeding 4.3 V (vs Li/Li<sup>+</sup>) is one of the origins for the degradation of high-voltage cells. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28−30)</a> The potential of Na is 0.33 V higher than that of lithium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 and 3">(1,3)</a> Accordingly, it is predicted that electrolytes would be oxidized continuously more than 4.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> For NVPF<sub>3</sub>, a long duration of 4.2 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) exists upon cycling, which lies outside the stable window of available electrolytes and would permit processive parasitic reactions to result in serious capacity attenuation.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among the strategies for inhibiting the oxidation of electrolytes, nitrile-based compounds are particularly attractive as additives or main solvent benefiting from intrinsically oxidation-stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 30 and 32 to 38">(30,32−38)</a> Especially, SN as main solvent coupling with DEC achieved the reduced solubility of N-rich interphase to acquire an upgraded cycling performance of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F cathode, while the formation of N-rich interphase always has been a mystery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Furthermore, the preferential chemisorption between nitrile molecules and transition metal physically expels solvents from intimate contact with the cathode <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 30 and 32 to 34">(30,32−34)</a> or inhibits the dissolution of transition metals in the cathode to obtain an improved cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> However, the absence of intuitive evidence except XPS and theoretical calculations for verifying the adsorption behavior derives another view of the oxidation of nitriles to form the cathode–electrolyte-interface (CEI). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35−37)</a> The latter originates from the combination of SN and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> to reduce the oxidation potential, only proved by DFT calculations. Obviously, there is still a lack of systematic mechanistic understanding and intuitive evidence of how nitriles improve the stability of high-voltage cathodes. Therefore, production chain behaviors of construction CEI in nitrile-based electrolytes should be verified from the solvation structure to interfacial adsorption and reactivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:DEC (1:1. vol) is selected as the base electrolyte to clarify that the persistent oxidation of electrolytes is the main reason for the serious capacity decay for NVPF<sub>3</sub>-based batteries, not the F-loss or the collapse of the crystal structure. Then, we designed a simple strategy based on electrolyte optimization by introducing additives with dual –C≡N groups such as succinonitrile (SN) to lift the capacity retention of NVPF<sub>3</sub>. Combining sum-frequency generation (SFG), in situ electrochemical surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy, etc., with theoretical calculations, an intensive and innovative preferential-adsorption and subsequent-decomposition mechanism of dinitriles is proposed (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>). With two –C≡N groups, an intermolecular hydrogen bond can be formed between one –C≡N group and solvents (–CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub>), enabling the dinitriles to be easily accessible to the solvation sheath through the other –C≡N group and subsequently lower the coordination numbers of solvents. Accordingly, the contact frequencies of solvents on the cathode surface can be decreased. Based on the modified solvation structure, the –C≡N group not entering the solvation layer has been proved to be preferentially absorbed prior to the –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> of solvents on NVPF<sub>3</sub> as barriers and oxidized as sacrificial agents to form a stable and electrically insulating CEI with rich CN<sup>–</sup>/NCO<sup>–</sup>/Na<sub>3</sub>N. Thence, an excellent capacity retention capability with lifted reversible capacity of NVPF<sub>3</sub> can be achieved. This work provides important guidance for achieving highly electrochemically stable SIBs, especially indispensable for the batteries assembling with high-voltage cathodes.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustration of Achieving High-Voltage Stability of NVPF<sub>3</sub> by Introducing Dual –C≡N Groups Containing Additives in the Electrolyte</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Capacity Loss Mechanism of Parasitic Oxidization of Electrolyte Exceeding 4.0 V</h3><div class="NLM_p">A NVPF<sub>3</sub>|NVP (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) cell was constructed to clarify the capacity fading mechanism of NVPF<sub>3</sub>-based batteries (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S4</a>). Results based on the chromatography and cycling performance in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>a, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5, and S6</a> show an obvious capacity fading rate of ∼15% (capacity loss of 15.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) with a tiny amount of 0.48% F<sup>–</sup> loss in NVPF<sub>3</sub> that can be observed after 500 cycles at 1 C. As calculated, 0.48% F<sup>–</sup> loss in NVPF<sub>3</sub> corresponds to a capacity loss of ∼0.61 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is far lower than the practical capacity loss after 500 cycles. Meanwhile, hardly detectable F<sup>–</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b), no obvious particle adhesion on the surface of cycled fiber film (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), and no peeling layer between active material and Al foil are observed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). Hence, the F<sup>–</sup> dissolution is not the main reason for capacity decay in NVPF<sub>3</sub>-based batteries.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) F-ions content in original and 500 cycled NVPF<sub>3</sub> obtained by ion chromatography, and ideal content by calculating. (b) Ion chromatography profiles of the glass fiber film. (c) In situ XRD contour plot of NVPF<sub>3</sub> in the 14–36° region. (d) Long cycling performance without supplementing Na<sup>+</sup> under 1 C. (e) Long cycling performance with supplementing Na<sup>+</sup> under 1 C. (f) Ex situ XRD profiles of the pristine NVPF<sub>3</sub> cathode and the cathode by replenishing Na<sup>+</sup>. (g) The linear scan voltammetry (LSV) profiles of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DEC, and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:DEC (1:1. vol). (h) The charge/discharge profiles of the NVPF<sub>3</sub>|NVP|Na three-electrode battery. (i) Capacity retention rate in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/diglyme at 2–3.8 V and 2–4.3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Meanwhile, in situ XRD during charging/discharging was used to verify the structural stability of NVPF<sub>3</sub>. The results show that the crystal of tetragonal NVPF<sub>3</sub> (<i>P</i>4<sub>2</sub>/<i>mnm</i>) follows the procedure of (<i>P</i>4<sub>2</sub>/<i>mnm</i>)–(<i>I</i>4/<i>mmm</i>)–(<i>Cmc</i>2<sub>1</sub>) during charging and (<i>Cmc</i>2<sub>1</sub>)–(<i>I</i>4/<i>mmm</i>)–(<i>P</i>4<sub>2</sub>/<i>mnm</i>) during discharging, indicating a highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation of NVPF<sub>3</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9</a>). After 500 cycles at 1 C, a serious capacity decay rate of over 30% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d,e) can be observed. When replenishing the Na-ion to the crystal (Na-ion from the anode can migrate to NVPF<sub>3</sub>), the capacity can be restored with a retention of over 95% several times, and accordingly, the cathode structure can be recovered in its pristine form, whereas that of the cycled NVPF<sub>3</sub> without supplementing the Na-ion is void and continues to decay (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>d–f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10</a>). The results indicate that the NVPF<sub>3</sub> cathode presents an excellent structural stability accompanied by an active sodium-deficient state after cycling. Actually, the EC-based electrolytes will be violently oxidized with a large consumption of the sodium-ion from the cathode when exceeding 4.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, resulting from a long duration (∼30 min at 1 C) at a high-voltage platform of 4.2 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for NVPF<sub>3</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>g,h). When avoiding 4.2 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> platform during cycling, the capacity retention can be significantly improved (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i). The above results strongly confirm that the capacity fading of NVPF<sub>3</sub> is primarily caused by the Na-ion loss of the NVPF<sub>3</sub> framework originated from the parasitic oxidation of electrolytes, especially EC at high-voltage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Therefore, the suppression of solvent breakdown would be crucial.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Validation of High-Voltage Protection by Dual –C≡N Groups Containing Additives</h3><div class="NLM_p">Thereafter, dinitriles like succinonitrile (SN) with dual –C≡N groups were chosen as additives to restrict the electrolyte oxidation, especially for solvents. First, the electrochemical behaviors based on the electrolyte of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:DEC (1:1. vol) (F-E-D) and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC:DEC (1:1. vol) with 2 wt % SN (F-E-D@SN) were investigated. The results of chronoamperometry lasting for 10–90 min at 4.2 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> show that the oxidation peak and constant oxidation current can be reduced by ∼1/6 and ∼1/3, respectively, after introducing SN (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b). The result preliminarily verifies the effective high-voltage endurance of the F-E-D@SN-based electrolyte.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Chronoamperometry profiles for electrolytes under 4.2 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for (a) 30, (b) 10, 30, 60, and 90 min. (c) Coulombic efficiency and discharge specific capacity of the first three cycles. The long cycling performances of NVPF<sub>3</sub>|NVP batteries with F-E-D and F-E-D@SN electrolytes under (d) 1.0 C, (e) 2.0 C, and (f) 5.0 C at 25 °C. (g) Power capability from 0.2 to 5 C. (h) The charge/discharge profiles from 0.2 to 5 C. (i) Diffusion coefficient of Na-ion obtained by cyclic voltammograms.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Subsequently, the electrochemical performance with the F-E-D@SN-based electrolyte was performed based on NVPF<sub>3</sub>|NVP cells at high-voltage operation (2–4.3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c shows that the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and capacity are 85.7% and 112.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when SN is introduced, respectively, which is higher than 82.5% and 105.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of the blank. In the following two cycles, the F-E-D@SN system exhibits more obvious disparity with higher Coulombic efficiency and capacity than the blank. Most notably, excellent long-term cycling stability can be observed with the SN-containing electrolyte, where the capacity retention rates maintain at 79.17, 98.4, and 93.0% for 1, 2, and 5 C after 1000 cycles, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d–f). Contrarily, the capacity retention was rather low for the F-E-D-based electrolyte (33.45, 72.0, and 76.8% at 1, 2, and 5 C, respectively). In addition to the EC-based electrolytes, the introduction of SN into PC-based electrolytes can also improve the cycling performance of the NVPF<sub>3</sub> cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>), such as in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/PC:EC (1:1.vol), a higher capacity retention of 98.07% with SN-adding than that of the system without additives (74.84%) can be observed after 500 cycles at 2 C.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Except for the cyclability, the cells with SN-containing electrolyte show a promotional high-rate capability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g,h). A high retention of 90.47% at 5 C (vs 0.2 C, 117.04 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and a complete capacity recovery from 5 to 0.2 C can be obtained after successive rate cycling for the designed electrolyte. Whereas, the capacity is decaying continuously and would not be restored mostly for the F-E-D system. The excellent kinetic features and stability could originate from the faster Na-ion diffusion and advantageous CEI for the F-E-D@SN-based electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12–S14</a>). These results prove that the SN additive is conducive to achieving a superiorly stable performance even at high-rates.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Solvation Modification and CEI Forming Based on Dual –C≡N Groups</h3><div class="NLM_p">After that, the molecular electrostatic potential (ESP) distribution of different constituents was probed first. The results show a positive potential for H of –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> on EC/DEC, a negative potential for N of –C≡N on SN, and the potential for O of –C═O on EC/DEC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). The reduced density gradient (RDG) further shows that there is an isosurface between N (–C≡N), O (–C═O), and H (–CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> in EC/DEC) in EC-SN, DEC-SN, and EC-DEC complexes, indicating a significant weak hydrogen bond interaction between chemicals <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–c). Meanwhile, a redshift of absorption frequency of –CH<sub>2</sub> from 2996.4 (pure EC) to 2990.6 cm<sup>–1</sup> in EC:SN (1:1. vol), –C≡N from 2253.9 (pure SN) to 2252.4 cm<sup>–1</sup> (EC-SN), and 2252.9 cm<sup>–1</sup> (DEC-SN), respectively, and the increased intensity in solvent-additives can be observed from Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), further confirming the existence of H-bonds. Ulteriorly, the binding energy of EC-SN (−10.40 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>) and DEC-SN (−8.20 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>) is stronger than that of EC-DEC complexes (−7.66 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>), indicating that solvents, especially EC, are more prone to form hydrogen bonds with additives. Thanks to the H-bond, the HOMO energy level of electrolytes can be reduced from −0.29 to −0.31 hartree for EC and −0.28 to −0.30 hartree for DEC, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e), which is desirable to improve the antioxidation property of electrolytes.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Gradient isosurfaces of (a) EC-SN, (b) DEC-SN, and (c) EC-DEC complexes obtained by the RDG method. (d) FTIR spectra of the chemicals in the region from 3100 to 2220 cm<sup>–1</sup>. (e) HOMO and LUMO energy of solvents, SN, and EC-SN, and DEC-SN complexes from DFT calculations based on molecular orbital theory. (f) <sup>13</sup>C nuclei NMR for electrolytes. (g) <sup>23</sup>Na nuclei NMR for electrolytes. (h) The charge of pure SN and EC-SN complexes with H-bonds. Raman spectra of (i) EC:DEC (1:1. vol)-based electrolyte and (j) EC:DEC (1:1. vol)@2 wt % SN-based electrolyte. (k) The coordination abundance value of the chemicals deconvoluted by Raman peaks. The color scheme in a–c, e, and h: bright blue, C atom; white, H atom; red, O atom; and dark blue, N atom.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Similar to the –C═O group, the electron-rich –C≡N group in SN can easily coordinate with metal-ions like Na-ion upon solvation and accordingly regulate the solvation structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 42 to 45">(42−45)</a> From <sup>13</sup>C nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f, the C signal of –C═O and –C≡N functionalities shifts downfield when NaPF<sub>6</sub> is added into the salt-free electrolyte (E-D with SN or without SN), demonstrating that an intermolecular interaction occurs between the chemicals and sodium-ions. This can result from the electron enrichment around the O and N nuclei. Simultaneously, the solvation interaction of SN-Na <sup>+</sup> can be enhanced by the H-bond of solvents-additives. The hydrogen bond forming with one of the terminal –C≡N groups is favorable to increase the electron density of the other –C≡N and then enhance the interaction of SN-Na<sup>+</sup>, which enables the SN molecules to enter into the solvation sheath more easily (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h). By comparison, there is a lower downfield shift (0.68 ppm for EC, 0.22 ppm for DEC) than the situation without SN (0.70 ppm for EC, 0.23 ppm for DEC), indicating a reduced interaction of solvent-Na<sup>+</sup> for the SN-adding system, especially EC-Na<sup>+</sup>. A downfield shift of the <sup>23</sup>Na spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g) also confirms the weakened solvent-Na<sup>+</sup> interaction due to the solvation of SN-Na<sup>+</sup> when introducing additives. Accordingly, the coordination capability of solvents can be effectively weakened by the additives with dual –C≡N groups.</div><div class="NLM_p">From the Raman spectroscopy, the peaks of coordinated EC (1817 cm<sup>–1</sup>), DEC (1754 cm<sup>–1</sup>), and SN (2268 cm<sup>–1</sup>) can be observed, when adding NaPF<sub>6</sub> into the salt-free electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>i,j and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S16</a>), further verifying the coordination between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents and additives. The ratios of solvated chemicals to the sum of free and solvated chemicals were used to quantify the relative abundance of solvated EC, DEC, and SN by deconvoluting the free/coordinated peaks. The abundance values can be calculated as 3.55 and 1.4 for EC and DEC, respectively, for the blank electrolyte, while they are 2.53 and 0.74 for the SN-containing system (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>k). The result indicates a decreased coordination capability of EC and DEC due to the presence of SN, which is favorable to abate the probability and frequency of solvents contacting the electrode surface and then potentially decelerate their parasitic oxidation during the high-voltage period.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the physical contact, more importantly, the modified solvation structure affects CEI formation and ultimately the electrochemical property of the batteries. For CEI processing, the step of adsorption and oxidation inside the electric double layer (EDL) is indispensable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> From SFG (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>), when adding NaPF<sub>6</sub> into the salt-free electrolyte, the signals of –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> (2800–3000 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and –CO (1700–1900 cm<sup>–1</sup>) groups on the interface are weakened, demonstrating that the solvents are squeezed out of the EDL under involving the solvation structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b). When adding SN into the F-E-D electrolyte, further weakening signals of the –CO group and, meanwhile, strong signals of the –CN group (2000–2300 cm<sup>–1</sup>) can be observed, which demonstrates a prior adsorption of SN to solvent molecules on the interface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). Moreover, the signal intensities of –CN functionality with 2 wt % SN-added electrolyte and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/SN:DEC (1:1.vol) are similar, indicating a dominant adsorption of SN molecules.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. SFG signals on the NVPF<sub>3</sub> surface at different wavenumber ranges: (a) –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> signals from 2700 to 3100 cm<sup>–1</sup>, (b) –CO signals from 1600 to 2000 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and (c) –CN signals from 2000 to 2400 cm<sup>–1</sup>. In situ EC-SERS profiles inside the EDL under different voltages of (d) F-E-D@SN electrolyte and (e) F-E-D electrolyte. (f) In situ EC-SERS profiles of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> inside the EDL from 800 to 450 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the F-E-D@SN electrolyte. The variation trend of –CH, –CO, –CN, and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> inside the EDL of F-E-D and F-E-D@SN electrolytes with respect to voltage (g) at OCV and initial stage of charging, (h) from OCV to 4.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. (i) Potential of zero charge of the NVPF<sub>3</sub> cathode. (j) Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) for F-E-D and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/SN electrolytes. (k) Absorption energy of chemicals. (l) HOMO energy of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-SN complexes (insets are the simulation snapshots). The color scheme in 4l is the same as that in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After that, in situ electrochemical surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy (EC-SERS) was performed to analyze the distribution of molecules inside the EDL during the charging process. From the characteristic peaks in EC-SERS, obtained by normalizing the surface concentration distribution as a function of the voltage, the –C≡N groups can be accumulated inside the EDL accompanied by excluding PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> from the interface as a voltage was applied (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>d,f,g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S18–S21</a>). The preferent adsorption of SN molecules compared to anions can be also verified by the shift of the potential of zero charge from 1.519 to 1.538 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> in the presence of additives in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i. While for the blank system, the –CH<sub>2</sub>/CH<sub>3</sub> groups were enriched on the interface, and –C═O groups were squeezed out of the EDL, manifesting a prior contact of –CH<sub>2</sub>/CH<sub>3</sub> than the –C═O group (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>e,g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S21</a>). As the potential increases to around 3.0 and 3.7 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h), a sharp decrease followed by a gradual decrease in the concentration of –C≡N groups originates from the oxidative decomposition of SN, which can be obtained by LSV with a 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/SN-based electrolyte (F-SN) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>j and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S22</a>). As for the blank electrolyte, a negligible concentration fluctuation of –CH<sub>2</sub>/CH<sub>3</sub> groups in solvents can be observed, and abundant solvent molecules are responsible for the continuous parasitic reactions.</div><div class="NLM_p">In summary, benefiting from the solvation through Na<sup>+</sup> ∼ N≡C– (one –C≡N) and Na<sup>+</sup> ∼ O═C–, the other –C≡N group in SN and the –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> group in solvents are exposed, resulting in a competitive adsorption of SN and solvent molecules on the interface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S23 and S24</a>). The results of adsorption energy demonstrate the most susceptible adsorption of SN molecules, which is especially obvious when applying an electric field (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>k and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S25</a>). The preferential oxidation of additives aggregated inside the EDL occurs originating from the higher HOMO energy level of Na<sup>+</sup>-SN (−0.438 hartree) than of Na<sup>+</sup>-EC (−0.453 hartree) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>l). Therefore, a mechanism of preferential adsorption and oxidation of SN can be obtained due to the modified solvation environment in the designed electrolyte.</div><div class="NLM_p">Subsequently, time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIM) was employed to clear the distribution of CEI decided by the decomposition priorities of electrolytes. Notably, it is observed that the majorities of C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>–</sup>/C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>–</sup> (parasitic compositions from solvents) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b) and NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>–</sup> (NaF, the product of NaPF<sub>6</sub>) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S26</a>) are evenly dispersed throughout the interface for the system with the F-E-D electrolyte. While for the SN-added system, a thinner CEI with dominant CN<sup>–</sup>/NCO<sup>–</sup> (chemical/electrochemical products of SN) along with weak C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>–</sup>/C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>–</sup> signals can be observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>a–d, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S27, and S28</a>). Consistently, a smaller fraction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na and a new component of Na<sub>3</sub>N can be observed from X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) for the targeted electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e,f), performing a different interface electrochemistry due to the prior adsorption and oxidation of SN molecules. The obtained CEI enriched with CN<sup>–</sup>/NCO<sup>–</sup>/Na<sub>3</sub>N components possesses high ionic conductivity and good electronic insulation, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> which is favorable to build an effective barrier between the electrolyte and cathode and hence improve the high-voltage tolerance. The gas in the F-E-D@SN-based electrolyte was greatly reduced based on an optical camera (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g,h), differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S29</a>), and the gas production of pouch-batteries by gas chromatography (GC) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S30</a>), further confirming the inhibition effect of SN additives.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Interfacial characterizations of the cycled cathodes in different electrolytes. TOF-SIMs 3D visualization of selected secondary-ion fragments (a) CN<sup>–</sup>, (b) NCO<sup>–</sup>, (c) C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>–</sup>, and (d) C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>–</sup>. XPS profiles for NVPF<sub>3</sub> cycled in investigated electrolytes: (e) C 1s and (f) N 1s spectra. Optical photos of the gas-bag of pouch-batteries cycled at 45 °C@0.05 C with the (g) F-E-D-based electrolyte and (h) F-E-D@SN-based electrolyte.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Verification of the Universality of Dual –C≡N Groups Containing Additives</h3><div class="NLM_p">Can other dinitriles containing two –C≡N groups with similar physical and chemical characteristics (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S31</a>) achieve an equal effect like SN? Consequently, a universal study of other dinitriles has been performed. The results show (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a–d) that the oxidation peak and constant oxidation current can be reduced by ∼1/6 and ∼1/2, respectively. The capacity retention rate of the cells with dinitrile-based electrolytes is above 88% [91.30, 97.02, and 88.32% for 1,4-dicyanobutane (DNB), 1,5-dicyanopentane (DNP), and 1,8-octanedinitrile (ON), respectively] after 500 cycles at 1 C, which is much higher than that of the F-E-D electrolyte (65.32%) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S32</a>). Remarkably, the capacity retention rate is still as high as 95.07% for F-E-D@DNP after 1000 cycles at 1 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S33</a>).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/medium/ja4c00702_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Chronoamperometry profiles for electrolytes at 4.2 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for 30 min. (b) Enlargement of (a) from 0 to 0.015 mA. (c) Chronoamperometry profiles for electrolytes at 4.2 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for 10, 30, 60, and 90 min. (d) Enlargement of (c) from −0.01 to 0.03 mA. (e) Cycling test of NVPF<sub>3</sub>|NVP cells with different electrolytes at 1 C@25 °C (500 cycles). (f) The 1000 cycles performance of NVPF<sub>3</sub>|NVP cells with the DNP-based electrolyte at 1 C@25 °C. (g) Raman spectra of EC:DEC (1:1. vol)@2 wt % ANN-based electrolyte. The variation trend of –CH, –CO, –CN, and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> inside the EDL of F-E-D@ANN electrolyte with respect to voltage (h) at OCV and initial stage of charging, (i) from OCV to 4.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. The color scheme in 6b-4d is the same as that in 6a.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/asset/images/large/ja4c00702_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c00702&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">However, no favorable consequence was achieved with mononitriles-adding system (acrylonitrile, ANN) (capacity retention rate of 73.65% after 500 cycles at 1 C in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S32</a>), resulting from the single –C≡N group, sacrificing the competitive adsorption advantage. Specifically, in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g, when adding 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> into the salt-free electrolyte (EC:DEC/1:1.vol), the peaks of coordinated ANN belonging to the –C≡N group (2245 cm<sup>–1</sup>) can be observed, which is more obvious in 2 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-added electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S34</a>). The resulting reduced coordination numbers of solvents would alleviate the anodic decomposition of electrolytes as the same as SN-added electrolyte, which achieves slightly higher capacity retention rate than the blank. But, in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a>h, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S35, and S36</a>, the –C≡N groups were squeezed out of the EDL for the F-E-D@ANN electrolyte, accompanied by the enrichment of solvents and anions, as a voltage was applied. The lower extrusion of anions can be further affirmed by the lower PZC of 1.430 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> of the NVPF<sub>3</sub> cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S37</a>) for the ANN-added system, compared to that of the F-E-D@SN and F-E-D system (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i). Simultaneously, with the potential increasing, a great deal of solvents adsorbed on the interface is continuously reduced (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>i) in the ANN-added system, which demonstrates that the solvents are involved in developing a loose and unstable interface in the F-E-D@ANN electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S38 and S39</a>). These results demonstrate the deprivation of the competitive adsorption advantage for the single –C≡N group in the ANN-based electrolyte. Therefore, all the above results confirm the universality of preferential adsorption and decomposition brought about by modified solvation structures of introducing dual –C≡N groups.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we deeply confirmed that the mainspring of capacity degradation for NVPF<sub>3</sub> is the oxidative decomposition of electrolytes under high-voltage (&gt;4.0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) rather than fluorine-ion dissolution and structural destruction of the cathode. Consequently, a new electrolyte with dual –C≡N groups additives like SN endows NVPF<sub>3</sub>-based sodium-ion cells (2–4.3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) with excellent stability and high-rate capability to guarantee the practical application of high-voltage cathodes. One –C≡N group can be involved in the solvation structure and enables the other –C≡N group in SN to be exposed and then squeezed into the EDL. Compared with the exposed –CH<sub>2</sub>/–CH<sub>3</sub> group in Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes, the squeezed –C≡N group can be preferentially adsorbed on NVPF<sub>3</sub> as barriers and oxidation as sacrificial agents to construct a stable CEI enriched CN<sup>–</sup>/NCO<sup>–</sup>/Na<sub>3</sub>N. Ultimately, the essential relation between the solvation structure and interfacial absorption/reaction behavior on the electrode surface was revealed at the molecular level. This universal mechanism can pilot an efficient guidance for achieving high electrochemically stable SIBs assembling with high-voltage cathode especially.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/jacs.4c00702?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/jacs.4c00702</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed description of the experimental procedures; electrochemical and spectroscopic characterizations; and theoretical calculations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c00702/suppl_file/ja4c00702_si_001.pdf">ja4c00702_si_001.pdf (26.34 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.4c00702" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b09835"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">de

                10.1021/acsami.5b09835</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D1867-1875%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26issue%3D3%26author%3DE.%2Bde%250Ala%2BLlave%26author%3DV.%2BBorgel%26author%3DK.-J.%2BPark%26author%3DJ.-Y.%2BHwang%26author%3DY.-K.%2BSun%26author%3DP.%2BHartmann%26author%3DF.-F.%2BChesneau%26author%3DD.%2BAurbach%26title%3DComparison%2Bbetween%2BNa-Ion%2Band%2BLi-Ion%2BCells%253A%2BUnderstanding%2Bthe%2BCritical%2BRole%2Bof%2Bthe%2BCathodes%2BStability%2Band%2Bthe%2BAnodes%2BPretreatment%2Bon%2Bthe%2BCells%2BBehavior%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.5b09835&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.4c00702&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.5b09835&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Comparison between Na-Ion and Li-Ion Cells: Understanding the Critical Role of the Cathodes Stability and the Anodes Pretreatment on the Cells Behavior</span></div><div class="casAuthors">de la Llave, Ezequiel; Borgel, Valentina; Park, Kang-Joon; Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Sun, Yang-Kook; Hartmann, Pascal; Chesneau, Frederick- Francois; Aurbach, Doron</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1867-1875</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. behavior of Na-ion and Li-ion full cells was investigated, using hard carbon as the anode material, and NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 and LiNi0.5Mn0.5O2 as the cathodes.  A detailed description of the structure, phase transition, electrochem. behavior and kinetics of the NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cathodes is presented, including interesting comparison with their lithium analog.  The crit. effect of the hard carbon anodes pretreatment in the total capacity and stability of full cells is clearly demonstrated.  Using impedance spectroscopy in three electrodes cells, we show that the full cell impedance is dominated by the contribution of the cathode side.  We discuss possible reasons for capacity fading of these systems, its connection to the cathode structure and relevant surface phenomena.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, S. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, N.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, K. T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">7</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2067</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2081</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c3ee40811f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D2067-2081%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26issue%3D7%26author%3DS.%2BY.%2BHong%26author%3DY.%2BKim%26author%3DY.%2BPark%26author%3DA.%2BChoi%26author%3DN.-S.%2BChoi%26author%3DK.%2BT.%2BLee%26title%3DCharge%2Bcarriers%2Bin%2Brechargeable%2Bbatteries%253A%2BNa%2Bions%2Bvs.%2BLi%2Bions%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc3ee40811f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.4c00702&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc3ee40811f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2067-2081</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1149/1.2756975"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barker, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gover, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, the sodium-ion battery is one of the hottest topics in the field of battery research.  For practical application, however, high tap d., which is a key point of electrode materials, has always been ignored in previous studies.  Here, we present the fabrication of a NASICON-type Na3V2(PO4)2F3@C/CNT composite cathode with a high tap d. of 1.2 g cm-3 for high power and long lifespan sodium-ion batteries.  The spherical NVPF@C/CNTs synthesized through a spray drying method are composed of micron-sized primary particles dispersed within the intertwined carbon nanotube matrix.  The CNT framework not only provides an efficient elec. highway between primary particles, but also forms a stable mech. backbone for secondary particles.  The initial discharge capacity of NVPF@C/CNTs is about 113 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 1C, and the cell retains 98.2% after 300 cycles.  Besides, a high rate capacity of 85 mA h g-1 has been achieved at 30C.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)2F3 is a pos. electrode material for Na-ion batteries which is attracting strong interest due to its high capacity, rate capability, and long-term cycling stability.  The sodium extn. mechanism from this material was always described in the literature as a straightforward solid soln., but several hints point toward a more complicated phase diagram.  In this work we performed high angular resoln. synchrotron radiation diffraction measurements, realized operando on sodium batteries upon charge.  We reveal an extremely interesting phase diagram, created by the successive crystn. of four intermediate phases before the end compn. NaV2(PO4)2F3 is reached.  Only one of these phases undergoes a solid soln. reaction, in the interval between 1.8 and 1.3 Na per formula unit.  The ability to resolve weak Bragg reflections allowed us to reveal differences in terms of symmetry among the phases, to det. their previously unknown space groups, and to correlate them with sodium (dis)ordering in the structure.  Rietveld refinements enabled us to follow fine structural modifications in great detail.  Intermediate identified phases are not simply described by their unit cell parameters, but bond-length variations can be tracked, as well as polyhedral distortions and site occupancy factors for mobile sodium ions.  For NaV2(PO4)2F3 a full crystal structure detn. was also carried out for the first time directly from operando measurements, assigning it to the Cmc21 space group and revealing two vanadium environments: V3+ and V5+.  Our study demonstrates that improved angular resoln. and high intensity diffraction data are key parameters for direct observation of fine reaction pathways in electrode materials and that the obtained insight is crucial for the understanding of (de)intercalation mechanisms in Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C1EE01263K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding and recent development of carbon coating on LiFePO<sub>4</sub> cathode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Olivine-structured LiFePO4 has been the focus of research in developing low cost, high performance cathode materials for lithium ion batteries.  Various processes have been developed to synthesize LiFePO4 or C/LiFePO4 (carbon coating on LiFePO4), and some of them are being used to mass produce C/LiFePO4 at the com. or pilot scale.  Due to the low intrinsic electronic and ionic conductivities of LiFePO4, the decrease of particle size and the nano-layer of carbon coating on LiFePO4 particle surfaces are necessary to achieve a high electrochem. performance.  Significant progress has been made in understanding and controlling phase purity, particle size and carbon coating of the C/LiFePO4 composite material in the past.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-ionicity fluorophosphate lattice via aliovalent substitution as advanced cathode materials in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Zhen-Yi; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Zhao, Xin-Xin; Wang, Xiao-Tong; Xie, Dan; Sun, Zhong-Hui; Zhao, Chen-De; Liang, Hao-Jie; Li, Wen-Hao; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">InfoMat</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">694-704</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">INFOHH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2567-3165</span>.

        </div><div class="casAbstract">As a cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF) with 3D open framework is a promising candidate due to its high working voltage and large theor. capacity.  However, the severe capacity degrdn. and poor rate capability hinder its practical applications.  The present study demonstrated the optimization of Na-storage performance of NVPF via delicate lattice modulation.  Aliovalent substitution of V3+ at Na+ in NVPF induces the generation of electronic defects and expansion of Na+-migration channels, resulting in the enhancement in electronic cond. and acceleration of Na+-migration kinetics.  It is disclosed that the formed stronger Na O bonds with high ionicity than V O bonds lead to the significant increase in structural stability and ionicity in the Na+-substituted NVPF (NVPF-Nax).  The aforementioned effects of Na+ substitution achieve the unprecedented electrochem. performance in the optimized Na3.14V1.93Na0.07(PO4)2F3 (NVPF-Na0.07).  As a result, NVPF-Na0.07 delivers a high-rate capability (77.5 mAh g-1 at 20 C) and ultralong cycle life (only 0.027% capacity decay per cycle over 1000 cycles at 10 C).  Sodium-ion full cells are designed using NVPF-Na0.07 as cathode and Se@reduced graphene oxide as anode.  The full cells exhibit excellent wide-temp. electrochem. performance from -25 to 25°C with an outstanding rate capability (96.3 mAh g-1 at 20 C).  Furthermore, it delivered an excellent cycling performance over 300 cycles with a capacity retention exceeding 90% at 0.5 C under different temps.  This study demonstrates a feasible strategy for the development of advanced cathode materials with excellent electrochem. properties to achieve high-efficiency energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c04647"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tahir, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saeed, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorine Dissolution-Induced Capacity Degradation for Fluorophosphate-Based Cathode Materials</span>. <i>ACS Appl.
